{'Reason_for_Visit': 'TASK: Extract 'Reason for Visit'.
Be SPECIFIC — include the patient's diagnosis, cancer type, and the purpose of this visit.
Do NOT give vague answers like "new symptoms" or "follow up visit". Instead, state what cancer,
what stage (if known), and what specifically brought them in (e.g., "initial consult for newly
diagnosed metastatic ER+ breast cancer" or "cycle 3 day 1 of irinotecan for metastatic TNBC").

For Patient type — determining "New patient" vs "Follow up":
- "New patient" if ANY of these apply:
  * Note says "New Patient Evaluation", "Consult Note", "consultation", "establish care", "referred by", or "second opinion"
  * This is the patient's FIRST visit to this oncologist/clinic (even if they had surgery or treatment elsewhere)
  * Post-surgery first visit to medical oncology to discuss adjuvant/systemic therapy
- "Follow up" ONLY if the patient has been seen by THIS provider/clinic before for ongoing management
- When in doubt, "New patient" is safer than "Follow up"

For second opinion — write "yes" ONLY if the note explicitly mentions "second opinion", "another opinion", or the patient was clearly referred for an independent review of their case. Do NOT guess.

Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
"Patient type": "either New patient or follow up",
"second opinion": "whether the visit is consultation/second opinion or not",
"in-person": "either Televisit or in-person. (note, video consult, televisit, telehealth are the same thing)",
"summary": "A specific summary including cancer type, stage if known, and purpose of this visit."
}
', 'Cancer_Diagnosis': 'TASK: Extract cancer diagnosis details.

For Type_of_Cancer, be SPECIFIC — include as much of the following as the note provides:
1. Histologic subtype: IDC (invasive ductal carcinoma), ILC (invasive lobular carcinoma), DCIS, mixed, etc.
2. Receptor status — ER, PR, and HER2. e.g., "ER+/PR+/HER2-"
3. If receptor status changed between original diagnosis and metastatic/recurrent biopsy, note BOTH.
- Example: "ER+/PR+/HER2- invasive ductal carcinoma" NOT just "breast cancer"
- Example with change: "Originally ER+/PR+/HER2-, metastatic biopsy ER+/PR-/HER2-"
Do NOT write just "breast cancer" or "MBC" — always include the subtype and receptor status when available in the note.
IMPORTANT: Search the ENTIRE note for receptor status — it may appear in PMH, Problem List, Pathology, HPI, or Diagnosis section, not just the Assessment. If ER/PR/HER2 status is stated ANYWHERE, include it. Do NOT omit HER2 status just because it appears only in the Past Medical History.

HER2 status rules — follow strictly:
- If the note explicitly states HER2 result (positive, negative, equivocal, IHC score, FISH result), write it.
- If HER2 was NOT tested or NOT mentioned in the note, write "HER2: not tested" — do NOT guess or assume HER2- or HER2+.
- DCIS (ductal carcinoma in situ) often does NOT have HER2 testing. Do NOT assume HER2- for DCIS unless explicitly stated.
- HER2 IHC scoring — use this table to determine HER2 status:
  IHC 0     = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 1+    = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 2+    = EQUIVOCAL — must check FISH/ISH result:
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 (or gene copies ≥ 6) = HER2 POSITIVE
    IHC 2+ with FISH ratio < 2.0 (or "FISH negative")  = HER2 NEGATIVE
  IHC 3+    = HER2 POSITIVE
- FISH interpretation (when reported without IHC):
  FISH ratio ≥ 2.0 = amplified = HER2 POSITIVE
  FISH ratio < 2.0 = not amplified = HER2 NEGATIVE
- IMPORTANT: If treatment includes Herceptin/trastuzumab/pertuzumab/T-DM1/T-DXd/Tykerb/lapatinib/TCHP/THP/ado-trastuzumab, the cancer is HER2+. Do NOT write HER2-.
- IMPORTANT: If the note says "triple negative" ANYWHERE (HPI, A/P, diagnosis), that means ER-/PR-/HER2-. Write HER2 as NEGATIVE even if the HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] or masked. "Triple negative" is a definitive classification.
- IMPORTANT: If HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] but the Assessment/Plan writes "[REDACTED] negative" in the receptor status line, infer HER2 = negative.

For Stage_of_Cancer:
- Write the AJCC/TNM stage if stated (e.g., "Stage IIA", "pT2N0M0", "Stage IV")
- If the patient was originally diagnosed at one stage but now has metastatic recurrence, write BOTH:
  e.g., "Originally Stage [X], now metastatic (Stage IV)"
  IMPORTANT: Only write "Originally Stage [X]" if the note EXPLICITLY states the original stage number (e.g., "Stage IIA at diagnosis"). If the note only says "metastatic recurrence" without specifying the original stage, write just "Metastatic (Stage IV)" — do NOT guess or invent the original stage.
- If distant metastases are documented, the current stage is Stage IV even if not explicitly stated.
- If the Assessment/Plan states a stage (e.g., "Stage I", "early stage"), extract it — do NOT write "Not mentioned in note".
- If tumor size and node status are available, infer the approximate stage (e.g., "2.3 cm, 0/3 LN negative — approximately Stage I-II").
- IMPORTANT: Node-negative ("node neg", "0/N LN+") with small tumor (≤2cm) = Stage I, NOT Stage II.
  Stage II requires either positive lymph nodes OR tumor >2cm. "Early stage node negative" = Stage I.
- Only write "Not mentioned in note" if there is truly NO staging, tumor size, or node information anywhere in the note.

Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
"Type_of_Cancer": "Specific cancer type with histologic subtype and receptor statuses when available. e.g., 'ER+/PR+/HER2- invasive ductal carcinoma'",
"Stage_of_Cancer": "AJCC stage, or original + current if recurred/progressed. e.g., 'Stage IIA' or 'Originally Stage [X], now metastatic (Stage IV)' — only include original stage if explicitly stated in the note",
"Distant Metastasis": "if there is distant metastasis (met). Yes, to where; No; or Not sure. Note: axillary lymph nodes are REGIONAL, not distant.",
"Metastasis": "if there is met, Yes (to where), No, or Not sure"
}

- PAST/COMPLETED (do NOT include): medications already finished or stopped.
  Look for: "completed", "s/p", "stopped", "discontinued", "held", "finished [N] cycles"
- STOPPED/DROPPED (do NOT include): medications recently discontinued.
  Look for: "recently dropped", "stopped due to", "discontinued", "held", "self D/C", "D/Ced"

Focus on CANCER-RELATED medications: chemotherapy, hormonal therapy, targeted therapy, immunotherapy, bone-protecting agents.

Do NOT include: eye drops, blood pressure meds, diabetes meds, vitamins, allergy meds, or other non-cancer medications — even if they appear in the medication list.

IMPORTANT EXCEPTION: Clinic-administered drugs (IV chemotherapy, injections like leuprolide/zoladex/lupron, infusions) are often NOT listed in the outpatient medication list.   * "we discussed starting..." = NOT current (discussion only)

', 'Treatment_Goals': 'TASK: Extract the goal/intent of cancer treatment.

Write the INTENT of the cancer treatment itself: "curative", "palliative", "risk reduction", "adjuvant", or "symptom management".
Do NOT write the purpose of this visit (e.g., "follow-up", "discuss treatment options", "review treatment plan").

FIRST — check the note for EXPLICIT intent words:
- If the note says "palliative" or "palliative intent" or "palliative care" → write "palliative". Done.
- If the note says "curative" or "curative intent" → write "curative". Done.
- If the note says "adjuvant" → this describes the treatment TYPE, not the GOAL. For non-metastatic cancer, adjuvant treatment has curative intent → write "curative". Only write "palliative" if metastatic.
- If the note explicitly states an intent, USE IT. Do NOT override the physician's own words.

ONLY if no explicit intent word is found, use this decision tree:
- Early-stage cancer + surgery + adjuvant chemo/radiation/endocrine therapy → "curative" or "adjuvant"
- Neoadjuvant chemotherapy before surgery for curable cancer → "curative" (NOT "neoadjuvant")
- Metastatic/Stage IV cancer → "palliative"
- DCIS only, no invasive cancer → "risk reduction"
- Completed curative treatment, now on surveillance endocrine therapy → "adjuvant" or "risk reduction"
- Do NOT write "risk reduction" for early-stage invasive cancer receiving adjuvant chemo — that is "curative" or "adjuvant".
- Do NOT write "neoadjuvant" as a goal — write "curative" instead.

Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"goals_of_treatment": "curative, palliative, adjuvant, risk reduction, or symptom management"
}
', 'Response_Assessment': 'TASK: Extract how the cancer is CURRENTLY responding to treatment.

Think step by step before answering:
1. Has this patient started any cancer treatment yet? (surgery alone does not count as "treatment" for response assessment purposes)
   IMPORTANT: Completed radiation therapy COUNTS as treatment. If the patient recently finished radiation, they HAVE received treatment — do NOT write "Not yet on treatment". "Not yet on treatment" is ONLY correct if no cancer treatment (chemo, radiation, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy) has been given at all.
   IMPORTANT: A patient who was on PREVIOUS treatment and now shows disease PROGRESSION or RECURRENCE — that IS a response assessment (the response is "progression"). Even if they are switching to a new regimen or between treatments, document the current disease status from imaging/labs.
   For LOCAL RECURRENCE: describe the recurrence factually (e.g., "local recurrence in left breast, 1.7cm IDC on excisional biopsy"). Do NOT write "cancer is not responding to treatment" — the patient may have discontinued treatment or completed it years ago. Focus on WHAT happened (recurrence), not WHY.
   IMPORTANT: For patients on adjuvant/maintenance therapy, "no evidence of recurrence" or "no evidence of disease" on exam or imaging IS a valid response assessment — it means the treatment is working.
   IMPORTANT — the patient IS on treatment if ANY of these clues appear:
   - "currently on [drug]", "taking [drug]", "continue [drug]", "on [drug] since [date]"
   - Mentions of chemotherapy cycles, off weeks, dose adjustments (e.g., "off week", "cycle 3", "due to start next cycle")
   - "labs okay to proceed with chemotherapy" or similar pre-cycle clearance
   - Current medications include any oncologic drug (chemo, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy)
   - Active medication list contains chemotherapy drugs (Xeloda, ixabepilone, paclitaxel, etc.)
   Do NOT be confused by "off week" — a patient on an off week between chemo cycles IS on treatment.
2. If yes, what treatment are they on? (chemo, endocrine therapy, targeted therapy, immunotherapy)
3. Is there any evidence of how the cancer is responding? Search the ENTIRE note for ANY of these:
   - Imaging: CT stable, PET response, scan results
   - Tumor markers: CA 15-3, CEA rising or falling
   - Physical exam: mass shrinking, "breaking up", palpable changes, skin changes improving
   - Clinical status: "labs okay to proceed" (implies stable enough to continue)
   - Symptom changes: "able to do more", "pain improving", "up and about"
   Even brief exam comments like "area appears to be breaking up" count as response evidence.
4. If no treatment started yet, write "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess."
5. If on treatment but no response data in the note, write "Not mentioned in note."

STRICT RULES — do NOT violate:
- Do NOT include future plans ("will start", "plan to", "she will have radiation").
- Do NOT include treatment discussions or recommendations.
- Do NOT use generic boilerplate phrases. Every claim must be traceable to a specific sentence in the note.
- Do NOT write "tolerating therapy well" or "symptoms improved" unless the note EXPLICITLY uses those exact words.
- Do NOT use the word "responding" or "response" unless you can cite SPECIFIC imaging or tumor marker evidence. "Labs okay" or "stable on exam" alone does NOT mean the cancer is "responding" — it means the cancer status is unknown or stable.
- IMPORTANT: "responding" implies MEASURABLE IMPROVEMENT. Use "stable disease" for unchanged findings, "no evidence of disease" for no measurable disease, and "responding" ONLY when there is documented tumor shrinkage or marker decrease.
- Do NOT describe pathology results (tumor size, grade, margins) as a "response" — pathology is a diagnosis, not a treatment response.
- Do NOT describe surgical recovery ("healing well", "recovering from surgery") as a response assessment.
- Do NOT describe risk estimates or Oncotype scores as a response — those are prognostic tools, not treatment responses.
- Do NOT write "Not applicable" or "Not specified".
Examples:
- GOOD: "CT stable, CA 15-3 decreased from 28 to 14"
- GOOD: "no evidence of disease recurrence on imaging and exam"
- GOOD: "PET-CT shows stable to slightly decreased metabolic activity of osseous metastases"
- GOOD: "Axillary LN decreased from 8cm to 2-3cm after 2 cycles AC"
- BAD: "tolerating therapy well, symptoms improved" (generic boilerplate)
- BAD: "She will continue on letrozole and have radiation" (this is a plan, not a response)
- BAD: "0.9 cm grade 1 IDC, margins negative" (this is pathology, not treatment response)
- BAD: "Healing well from surgery" (surgical recovery, not cancer treatment response)
- BAD: "Low Risk Oncotype score" (prognostic tool, not a response to treatment)

Write your reasoning first, then output the JSON on a new line starting with {
IMPORTANT: Your JSON must contain ONLY the key "response_assessment". Do NOT add any other keys.

Respond with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"response_assessment": "How is the cancer CURRENTLY responding? Cite specific evidence: imaging, labs, exam. Do NOT include future plans, surgical recovery, or prognostic scores."
}
'}

- DO NOT include imaging (CT, PET, MRI, X-ray, ultrasound) or routine labs (CBC, CMP).

4. CRITICAL — do NOT include content that belongs in OTHER fields:
- Treatment contingency plans (e.g., "if progression, could use faslodex") → these go in Medication_Plan/Therapy_plan
- Imaging plans (PET CT, MRI) → these go in Imaging_Plan
- Response assessment text (e.g., "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess") → this is NOT genetic testing

5. If there is NO new genetic or molecular test planned in the Assessment/Plan:
- Set the value to: "None planned."

6. Output format:
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
    "genetic_testing_plan": "A summary of any future planned genetic or molecular tests, or a clear statement that none are planned."
}
', 'Referral': 'TASK: Extract 'Referral' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.
Do not mistake the doctor's signature at the end of the note as a referral.
Include ALL referrals TO OTHER providers/departments, including integrative medicine, complementary medicine, or wellness referrals.
A "consult" (e.g., "XRT consult", "Rad Onc consult") counts as a Specialty referral.

IMPORTANT: Look for referrals throughout the ENTIRE note, not just the A/P section. Referrals may appear in:
- HPI: "referred to genetics", "will refer to social work"
- Diagnosis/Problem List section: "Ambulatory Referral to Social Work", "Referral to Exercise Counseling"
- Psychologic/Social section: "I will refer to social work"
- A/P: "consult with Rad Onc", "nutrition consult", "dental evaluation"
Search for ALL of these keywords: "refer", "referral", "consult", "counseling", "social work", "exercise", "dental", "physical therapy", "PT", "OT", "psychiatry", "psychology", "palliative".
Common referrals found OUTSIDE the A/P section:
- "Ambulatory Referral to Social Work" (in Diagnosis section) → Others: Social work referral
- "Referral to Exercise Counseling" (in Diagnosis section) → Others: Exercise counseling referral
- "I will refer to social work" (in Psychologic section) → Others: Social work referral

CRITICAL — distinguish OUTGOING from INCOMING referrals:
- OUTGOING (include): This note's doctor is SENDING the patient elsewhere. E.g., "I referred her to Rad Onc", "consult with genetics recommended", "will schedule Rad Onc consult".
- INCOMING (do NOT include): The patient was SENT TO this doctor by someone else. E.g., "referred by Dr. Smith", "referred to us for evaluation", "referred for consideration of adjunct therapy".
- Do NOT include the current visit's own department. These notes are written by Medical Oncology — do NOT list "Medical Oncology" or "Med Onc" as a referral.
- If the note says "referred to Med Onc (today's visit) and also Rad Onc", only include "Rad Onc" — the Med Onc part describes THIS visit, not an outgoing referral.

If no referral is mentioned for a category, set its value to "None".
For Nutrition: only include referrals to a nutritionist or dietitian (e.g., "nutrition consult", "refer to nutritionist", "follow up with nutrition on [date]").
The following are NOT nutrition referrals — they are general lifestyle advice from the oncologist:
- "I recommend an anti inflammatory/mediterranean diet" → NOT a referral
- "I recommend a diabetes risk reducing diet" → NOT a referral
- "eat calcium-rich food", "limit alcohol", "exercise" → NOT a referral
- "take calcium and vitamin D", "continue vitamin D daily" → NOT a referral (supplement advice)
- "discussed lifestyle modifications" → NOT a referral
If the oncologist gives diet advice without referring to a nutritionist/dietitian, write "None".
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
"Nutrition": "any nutrition referrals such as diet optimization, appetite, weight maintenance, or 'None'",
"Genetics": "ONLY outgoing referrals TO a genetics clinic or counselor (e.g., 'refer to genetics', 'genetics consult recommended'). Do NOT list genetic test RESULTS or known mutations here (e.g., 'BRCA1 mutation', 'ATM mutation carrier', 'genetic testing negative' are FINDINGS, not referrals). Write 'None' if no genetics referral is planned.",
"Specialty": "eg, Palliative care, Radiation oncology consult, surgical oncology, Integrative Medicine, Psychology/psychiatry, Dental evaluation, or 'None'",
"Others": "Physical or occupational therapy, Exercise counseling referral, Social work, financial counseling, home health, or 'None'. IMPORTANT: General advice about exercise ('continue exercise', 'weight bearing exercise', 'discussed exercise') is NOT an exercise referral. Only include if there is an explicit referral to an exercise counseling program (e.g., 'Referral to Exercise Counseling').",
"follow up": "follow up mentioned in the provided text. f/up, f/p and fup all mean follow up."
}
', 'follow_up_next_visit': 'TASK: Extract the 'follow up/next visit' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.
Be SPECIFIC about timing (e.g., "2 weeks", "after biopsy results", "3 months").
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
    "Next clinic visit": "(in-person or telehealth): timing and purpose"
}
', 'Advance_care_planning': 'TASK: Extract the 'Advance care planning' from the clinical note.
Include Advance directives, health-care proxy, code status (e.g., Full code, DNR/DNI), living will.
IMPORTANT: Code status is often found OUTSIDE the Assessment/Plan section — look in the FULL NOTE for:
- "Code status: Full code" or "Full code"
- "DNR" or "DNI" or "DNR/DNI"
- "Advance Care Planning" section
- "has a living will" or "health care proxy"
If a code status is mentioned ANYWHERE in the note, include it.
If no advance care planning is discussed and no code status is found, say "Not discussed during this visit."
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
    "Advance care": "A summary of any advance care planning including code status, or 'Not discussed during this visit.'"
}

On exam 1 cm firm, mobile, nontender mass approximately 5 cm from nipple at 1 o'clock.  03/17/2019: Right breast FNA at 1 o'clock (UCSF): Benign and could be seen in the setting of fibrocystic change or fibroadenoma.  03/17/2018 Abdomen-pelvic CT: No acute findings (for abdominal pain).  03/28/2019 Bilateral breast diagnostic mammogram (UCSF): A focal asymmetry is suggested in the region. Triangular marker denotes site of focal pain in the lower slightly outer right breast, with no underlying mammographic correlate. Scattered and occasionally grouped benign-appearing microcalcifications, both breasts, predominantly coarse dystrophic, likely the sequelae of reported fat grafting.  03/28/2019 Bilateral breast diagnostic US(UCSF): 22 x 10 x 15 mm oval complex solid and cystic mass with peripheral echogenicity and an irregular, antiparallel hypoechoic central component in the right upper inner quadrant, 1:00, 8 cm from nipple. The central hypoechoic component measures up to 10 mm. No sonographic correlate to site of focal pain in the lower outer right breast at 7 o'clock, 5 cm from nipple. No sonographic correlate to clinician's site of concern in the left axilla. --- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did I come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a follow-up visit regarding your breast cancer treatment.\n**What was discussed?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which means the cancer started in the milk ducts. It is also ER+/PR+, meaning the cancer cells have receptors for the hormones estrogen and progesterone.\n**What tests were done or ordered?**\nYour blood tests are mostly normal. Some specific tests were done, including estradiol and vitamin D levels.\nEstradiol will be checked monthly.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou started taking letrozole today. You will continue a medication for at least 3 years and can switch to tamoxifen later. Gabapentin is prescribed as needed.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou are referred to your doctor for genetics counseling.\nYou should come back for another visit in 3 months or sooner if needed.\nWe noticed you appear anxious. Please let us know if you need support.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did I come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation to discuss treatment options and to establish care after receiving an incomplete course of neoadjuvant TCHP and a lumpectomy/ALND.\n**What was discussed?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which started in the milk ducts. It is HER2 positive and both estrogen and progesterone receptor negative. Your cancer was originally in an early to locally advanced stage, but it is now considered stage III. No distant spread of the cancer was found.\n**What tests were done or ordered?**\nYour recent tests included a lumpectomy and removal of lymph nodes, which showed no remaining cancer in the breast tissue but some cancer in the lymph nodes. Imaging tests like PET/CT scans did not show any spread of the cancer.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are currently taking oxyCODONE for pain.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will start a treatment plan called adjuvant AC, followed by T-DM1. This is to prevent the cancer from coming back. You will also need radiation after completing AC. You are referred to social work for additional support.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

She has consented to treatment and thus letrozole has been sent to ***** *****. Palbociclib has been sent to the ***** ***** *****. We discussed potential palbociclib side effects including but not limited to myelosuppression in particular neutropenia requiring count checks every 4 weeks before the start of the next cycle, as well as mucositis, fatigue, n/v, hair thinning, pneumonitis.      Since she has extensive bony mets and new pain, we recommend obtaining new scans and ordered MRI of total spine and CAP CT. We will request her outside PET/CT for comparison. We also discussed that in patients with breast cancer with bone metastases, use of bone modifying agents reduce the risk of skeletal related events. Negative 1+: Incomplete membrane staining that is faint/barely  perceptible in >10% of tumor cells. Large, enhancing hypermetabolic right breast mass measuring approximately 9.0 cm in maximal dimension, suggesting disease recurrence. Please see MR breast 12/11/2016 for further details.    2. Enhancing hypermetabolic right axillary and right subcutaneous lymph nodes, suggesting nodal involvement.    3. FDG uptake at the level of the coccyx is favored to be inflammatory or posttraumatic. Recommend correlation and attention on follow-up.    This study was reviewed by attendings Dr. ***** ***** from ***** ***** and Dr. ***** *****. ***** of Nuclear Medicine.    END OF IMPRESSION    Assessment and Recommendations:  64 y.o. postmenopausal patient with a clinical stage II-III *****-/*****+ IDC of the right breast who should proceed with neoadjuvant chemotherapy.    We reviewed the available imaging and pathology reports.  We discussed her original DCIS diagnosis from 2007 as well as a second biopsy in 2013 that was concerning for microinvasive disease; she did not undergo conventional therapy at the time of her original diagnoses.  I \n explained to her that she now had clear evidence of invasive breast cancer, but noted that her PET/CT demonstrated no evidence of metastases and therefore she can now be treated with curative intent.  I discussed with ***** and her husband in great detail the natural history and treatment of early stage *****-/*****+ IDC.  I explained that were she to forego the recommended therapy, I am very concerned that she would develop metastatic disease in the short term, even in the context of her 10 year history of DCIS.    We discussed the multimodality approach to breast cancer treatment. The patient understands that her treatment recommendations will include chemotherapy, biological therapies (trastuzumab and pertuzumab), surgery, and radiation. We discussed the advantages of offering chemotherapy in the neoadjuvant setting in order to increase the chances of breast conserving surgery, while concurrently allowing us to assess the response to treatment.  I explained that in the context of her high-risk node-negative, ***** positive disease we would typically recommend that she receive either 12 cycles of weekly paclitaxel plus trastuzumab/pertuzumab (THP) followed by 4 cycles of doxorubicin plus cyclophosphamide (AC) every 2 weeks or 6 cycles of every 3 week docetaxel, carboplatin, trastuzumab, and pertuzumab (TCHP).  I also explained that either regimen would be followed by every 3 week trastuzumab for a total of one year of biological therapy.     We reviewed the potential side effects of both regimens.  Given her extensive disease even in the absence of nodal involvement, I recommend that she proceed with an anthracycline-containing regimen, unless she is found to have substantial cardiac dysfunction on TTE.  I specifically discussed with ***** the typical and less common side-effects associated with THP/AC, such as: fatigue, nausea, myelosuppression, nail changes, alopecia, diarrhea, and constipation. I stated that \n with regards to trastuzumab, she is at risk of a reduced left ventricular ejection fraction, which can result in asymptomatic or symptomatic heart failure with dyspnea and chest pain.  I stated that pertuzumab is associated with diarrhea and rash.  We additionally discussed the rare adverse events associated with THP/AC, both secondary leukemia and cardiomyopathy.      After an extensive discussion, the patient completely understands the rationale for neoadjuvant treatment and will spend the weekend considering where she wants to proceed with her therapy.  I explained to her that I would recommend she start her treatment within 2 to 3 weeks.  She would need a TTE, Mediport placement, and if treated here at *****, a chemotherapy teaching session.    The patient will contact me after the weekend and let me know whether she plans to proceed with treatment here at ***** or elsewhere, closer to home.    I spent a total of 120 minutes face-to-face with the patient and 115 minutes of that time was spent counseling regarding the diagnosis, the treatment plan, the prognosis, medication risks, lifestyle modification, the risks and benefits of surgery, symptoms and therapeutic options.    \n"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did I come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation to discuss treatment options for your recent diagnosis of early stage breast cancer.\n**What was discussed?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which means the cancer started in the milk ducts. It is in the early stage, but it is considered stage II-III. The goal of treatment is to cure the cancer.\n**What tests were done or ordered?**\nYour blood test for creatinine was normal. You had a PET/CT scan that showed a large mass in your right breast and some lymph nodes that might be affected. No cancer was found in other parts of your body.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou will start treatment with chemotherapy and biological therapies to shrink the cancer before surgery. This is called neoadjuvant treatment. The plan is to use either paclitaxel with trastuzumab and pertuzumab, followed by doxorubicin and cyclophosphamide, or docetaxel, carboplatin, trastuzumab, and pertuzumab. You will also have trastuzumab for a year after the initial treatment.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will have a Mediport placed to make it easier to give you the chemotherapy. You also need an echocardiogram (TTE) before starting treatment. You will contact us after the weekend to decide where you want to receive your treatment.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

During the trial, patients will undergo one additional core biopsy of the breast mass in order to assess the biological changes that have occurred in the cancer in response to the ***** therapy plus chemotherapy. Patients will also undergo additional MRIs during the course of neoadjuvant chemotherapy to assist in assessing response to treatment. We discussed the very ***** trial design and explained that if the patient is randomized to the non-paclitaxel \n containing arm (such as ***** or *****), they can switch back to paclitaxel if not responding or receive it post operatively. In any of the arms, if the patient appears to have progression of her tumor either by physical exam or breast imaging, there is the option of discontinuing the study treatment and either moving on to the AC or switching to paclitaxel.     We reviewed the anticipated side effects and toxicities of chemotherapy including increased risk of infection, hair loss, fatigue, and possibility of ovarian failure. We explained that anthracyclines are associated with a small chance of causing permanent damage to the heart (estimated to be about 1% with 4 cycles of adriamycin and cyclophosphamide) and that ***** are associated with development of neuropathy which is most often transient and rarely permanent.    We also mentioned that given she was ER/PR low positive, we would still recommend 5-10 years of endocrine therapy after surgery. We did not discuss the options in length today, but can do so at a later date.    She asked about whether she could continue to work as an ICU nurse during chemo. We advised her that many patients choose to work during weekly taxol, but it will largely depend on which arm she is randomized to, as some of the experimental arms are likely to be associated with more toxicity. Most patients choose to take off time during AC and then after recovering from chemotherapy.     Plan:  -- ***** order TTE  -- Port placement  -- Chemotherapy teaching session  -- She is interested in screening for ISPY; then will need research core biopsy, research breast MRI, labs. F/u pending *****.   -- F/u results of genetic testing (done locally last Mon)  -- After surgery, she will be considered for post operative radiation and we will recommend 5-10yrs of adjuvant endocrine therapy     RTC in 1-2 weeks in person to start chemo    Patient seen and discussed with breast oncology attending \n Dr. ***** *****.     ***** *****, MD PGY-5  Hematology/Oncology Fellow    \n"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did I come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a consultation regarding your recent diagnosis of right breast cancer.\n**What was discussed?**\nYou have a type of breast cancer called invasive ductal carcinoma, which started in the milk ducts. The cancer is weakly positive for estrogen receptors and low positive for progesterone receptors, and it is negative for HER2. You also have cancer in the lymph nodes under your arm, but there is no evidence of the cancer spreading to other parts of your body.\n**What tests were done or ordered?**\nYou had imaging tests like MRI and PET/CT that showed a tumor in your right breast and some enlarged lymph nodes. A biopsy of the lymph node showed cancer cells. No blood tests were done during this visit.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are being considered for neoadjuvant chemotherapy, which is treatment given before surgery to shrink the cancer. The treatment options include a combination of drugs like doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, and paclitaxel. You may also be eligible for a clinical trial that tests new treatments. After surgery, you will likely need radiation therapy and a long-term medication to prevent the cancer from coming back.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will need a port placed for your chemotherapy, and you will have a biopsy and an MRI for research purposes. You will also have surgery to remove the tumor, which could be a lumpectomy or a mastectomy. You are referred to a genetic counselor to review the results of your genetic testing. You will have another visit in 1-2 weeks to start your chemotherapy.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"

--- Column: letter ---
"Dear Patient,\nWe hope you are doing well. We are writing this letter to help you understand what happened during your recent visit.\n**Why did I come to the clinic?**\nYou came in for a new patient evaluation regarding your ongoing management of metastatic breast cancer.\n**What was discussed?**\nYou have a history of breast cancer that started in the milk ducts (invasive ductal carcinoma) and has spread to other parts of your body, including your brain, lungs, and lymph nodes. The cancer in your breast was sensitive to certain hormones, but the cancer that spread to your brain is not.\n**What tests were done or ordered?**\nNo new tests were done during this visit.\n**What treatment or medication changes were made?**\nYou are currently taking a medication called Xeloda. If tests show that the cancer is sensitive to a protein called HER2, you may need additional treatment targeting this protein.\n**What is the plan going forward?**\nYou will continue taking Xeloda. Your doctor will check how you are doing after three months of treatment. If the cancer gets worse while you are on Xeloda, you may be considered for clinical trials or other treatments that combine immunotherapy with chemotherapy. You are also referred to have tests done on the cancer in your brain and the remaining cancer in your breast to check for sensitivity to hormones and the HER2 protein.\nThank you for trusting us with your care.\nPlease feel free to contact us if you have any questions.\nSincerely, Your Care Team"
